<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Photosynthesis</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Biological process to convert light into chemical energy</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photosynthesis.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1518" data-file-width="1094" height="305" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Photosynthesis.gif/220px-Photosynthesis.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Photosynthesis.gif/330px-Photosynthesis.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Photosynthesis.gif/440px-Photosynthesis.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photosynthesis.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Schematic of photosynthesis in plants. The carbohydrates produced are stored in or used by the plant.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Photosynthesis_equation.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="133" data-file-width="923" height="58" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Photosynthesis_equation.svg/400px-Photosynthesis_equation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Photosynthesis_equation.svg/600px-Photosynthesis_equation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Photosynthesis_equation.svg/800px-Photosynthesis_equation.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Photosynthesis_equation.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overall equation for the type of photosynthesis that occurs in plants</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="819" data-file-width="1280" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg/400px-Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg/600px-Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg/800px-Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seawifs_global_biosphere.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Composite image showing the global distribution of photosynthesis, including both oceanic <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> and terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Vegetation" title="Vegetation">vegetation</a>. Dark red and blue-green indicate regions of high photosynthetic activity in the ocean and on land, respectively.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Photosynthesis</b> is a process used by plants and other organisms to convert <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> energy into <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">chemical energy</a> that can later be <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">released</a> to fuel the organisms' activities (<a href="/wiki/Energy_transformation" title="Energy transformation">energy transformation</a>). This chemical energy is stored in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbohydrates" title="Carbohydrates">carbohydrate molecules</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugars</a>, which are synthesized from <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> – hence the name <i>photosynthesis</i>, from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%86%E1%BF%B6%CF%82" title="wiktionary:φῶς">φῶς</a>, <i>phōs</i>, "light", and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%83%CF%8D%CE%BD%CE%B8%CE%B5%CF%83%CE%B9%CF%82" title="wiktionary:σύνθεσις">σύνθεσις</a>, <i>synthesis</i>, "putting together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OnlineEtDict_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtDict-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ1_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSJ2_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSJ2-3">[3]</a></sup> In most cases, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> is also released as a waste product. Most <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plants</a>, most <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> perform photosynthesis; such organisms are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoautotroph" title="Photoautotroph">photoautotrophs</a>. Photosynthesis is largely responsible for producing and maintaining the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_oxygen" title="Atmospheric oxygen">oxygen content</a> of the Earth's atmosphere, and supplies all of the organic compounds and most of the energy necessary for <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life on Earth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryantfrigaard_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-bryantfrigaard-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although photosynthesis is performed differently by different species, the process always begins when energy from light is absorbed by <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> called <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_reaction_centre" title="Photosynthetic reaction centre">reaction centres</a> that contain green <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> pigments. In plants, these proteins are held inside <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> called <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>, which are most abundant in leaf cells, while in bacteria they are embedded in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasma_membrane" title="Plasma membrane">plasma membrane</a>. In these light-dependent reactions, some energy is used to strip <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electrons</a> from suitable substances, such as water, producing oxygen gas. The hydrogen freed by the splitting of water is used in the creation of two further compounds that serve as short-term stores of energy, enabling its transfer to drive other reactions: these compounds are reduced <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide_phosphate" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate">nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate</a> (NADPH) and <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">adenosine triphosphate</a> (ATP), the "energy currency" of cells.
</p><p>In plants, algae and cyanobacteria, long-term energy storage in the form of sugars is produced by a subsequent sequence of <span class="nowrap">light-independent</span> reactions called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin_cycle" title="Calvin cycle">Calvin cycle</a>; some bacteria use different mechanisms, such as the <a href="/wiki/Reverse_Krebs_cycle" title="Reverse Krebs cycle">reverse Krebs cycle</a>, to achieve the same end. In the Calvin cycle, atmospheric carbon dioxide is <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">incorporated</a> into already existing organic carbon compounds, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ribulose_bisphosphate" title="Ribulose bisphosphate">ribulose bisphosphate</a> (RuBP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-321-73975-2_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-321-73975-2-5">[5]</a></sup> Using the ATP and NADPH produced by the light-dependent reactions, the resulting compounds are then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_reductant" title="Biological reductant">reduced</a> and removed to form further carbohydrates, such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>.
</p><p>The first photosynthetic organisms probably <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> early in the <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">evolutionary history of life</a> and most likely used <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, rather than water, as sources of electrons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16453059_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16453059-6">[6]</a></sup> Cyanobacteria appeared later; the excess oxygen they produced contributed directly to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_catastrophe" title="Oxygen catastrophe">oxygenation of the Earth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18468984_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18468984-7">[7]</a></sup> which rendered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolution_of_multicellularity" title="Evolution of multicellularity">evolution of complex life</a> possible. Today, the average rate of energy capture by photosynthesis globally is approximately 130 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terawatts" title="Terawatts">terawatts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid10670014_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10670014-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitmarsh_Govindjee_1999_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitmarsh_Govindjee_1999-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Steger_2005_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Steger_2005-10">[10]</a></sup> which is about three times  the current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" title="World energy resources and consumption">power consumption of human civilization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EIA_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-11">[11]</a></sup>
Photosynthetic organisms also convert around 100–115 thousand million tonnes of carbon into <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9657713_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9657713-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ps-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1027" data-file-width="830" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/220px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/330px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/440px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photosynthesis changes sunlight into chemical energy, splits water to liberate O<sub>2</sub>, and fixes CO<sub>2</sub> into sugar.</div></div></div>
<p>Photosynthetic organisms are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoautotroph" title="Photoautotroph">photoautotrophs</a>, which means that they are able to <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthesize</a> food directly from carbon dioxide and water using energy from light. However, not all organisms use carbon dioxide as a source of carbon atoms to carry out photosynthesis; <a href="/wiki/Photoheterotroph" title="Photoheterotroph">photoheterotrophs</a> use organic compounds, rather than carbon dioxide, as a source of carbon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryantfrigaard_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-bryantfrigaard-4">[4]</a></sup> In plants, algae, and cyanobacteria, photosynthesis releases oxygen. This is called <i>oxygenic photosynthesis</i> and is by far the most common type of photosynthesis used by living organisms. Although there are some differences between oxygenic photosynthesis in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plants" title="Plants">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, the overall process is quite similar in these organisms. There are also many varieties of <a href="/wiki/Anoxygenic_photosynthesis" title="Anoxygenic photosynthesis">anoxygenic photosynthesis</a>, used mostly by certain types of bacteria, which consume carbon dioxide but do not release oxygen.
</p><p>Carbon dioxide is converted into sugars in a process called <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>; photosynthesis captures energy from sunlight to convert carbon dioxide into <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a>. Carbon fixation is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction. In general outline, photosynthesis is the opposite of <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>: while photosyntesis is a process of reduction of carbon dioxide to carbohydrate, cellular respiration is the oxidation of carbohydrate or other <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> to carbon dioxide. Nutrients used in cellular respiration include carbohydrates, amino acids and fatty acids. These nutrients are oxidized to produce carbon dioxide and water, and to release chemical energy to drive the organism's <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. Photosynthesis and cellular respiration are distinct processes, as they take place through different sequences of chemical reactions and in different <a href="/wiki/Cellular_compartment" title="Cellular compartment">cellular compartments</a>.
</p><p>The general <a href="/wiki/Chemical_equation" title="Chemical equation">equation</a> for photosynthesis as first proposed by <a href="/wiki/C._B._van_Niel" title="C. B. van Niel">Cornelis van Niel</a> is therefore:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitmarsh_1999_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitmarsh_1999-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">CO<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbon<br/>dioxide</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">2H<sub>2</sub>A</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">electron donor</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photons" title="Photons">photons</a></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">light energy</span></span></span></span> → <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">[​CH<sub>2</sub>O​]</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a></span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">2A</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">oxidized<br/>electron<br/>donor</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">H<sub>2</sub>O</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">water</span></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Since water is used as the electron donor in oxygenic photosynthesis, the equation for this process is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">CO<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbon<br/>dioxide</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">2H<sub>2</sub>O</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">water</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">photons</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">light energy</span></span></span></span> → <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">[CH<sub>2</sub>O]</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbohydrate</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">O<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">oxygen</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">H<sub>2</sub>O</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">water</span></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>This equation emphasizes that water is both a reactant in the <a href="#Light-dependent_reactions">light-dependent reaction</a> and a product of the <a href="#Light-independent_reactions">light-independent reaction</a>, but canceling <i>n</i> water molecules from each side gives the net equation:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">CO<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbon<br/>dioxide</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">H<sub>2</sub>O</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"> water </span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">photons</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">light energy</span></span></span></span> → <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">[CH<sub>2</sub>O]</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbohydrate</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">O<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"> oxygen </span></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<p>Other processes substitute other compounds (such as <a href="/wiki/Arsenite" title="Arsenite">arsenite</a>) for water in the electron-supply role; for example some microbes use sunlight to oxidize arsenite to <a href="/wiki/Arsenate" title="Arsenate">arsenate</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The equation for this reaction is:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">CO<sub>2</sub></span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbon<br/>dioxide</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">(AsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>arsenite</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">photons</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">light energy</span></span></span></span> → <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">(AsO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>)</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;"><br/>arsenate</span></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap"><span style="display:none;display:inline-block; vertical-align:top; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block;padding:0 0.1em;">CO</span><span style="display:block; font-size:70%; line-height:1em;padding:0 0.1em;"><span style="position:relative; line-height:1em; margin-top:-0.5em; top:-0.5em;">carbon<br/>monoxide</span></span></span></span>(used to build other compounds in subsequent reactions)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18703741_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18703741-16">[16]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Photosynthesis occurs in two stages. In the first stage, <i>light-dependent reactions</i> or <i>light reactions</i> capture the energy of light and use it to make the energy-storage molecules <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADPH" title="NADPH">NADPH</a>. During the second stage, the <i>light-independent reactions</i> use these products to capture and reduce carbon dioxide.
</p><p>Most organisms that utilize oxygenic photosynthesis use <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible light</a> for the light-dependent reactions, although at least three use shortwave <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> or, more specifically, far-red radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some organisms employ even more radical variants of photosynthesis. Some <a href="/wiki/Archaea" title="Archaea">archaea</a> use a simpler method that employs a pigment similar to those used for vision in animals. The <a href="/wiki/Bacteriorhodopsin" title="Bacteriorhodopsin">bacteriorhodopsin</a> changes its configuration in response to sunlight, acting as a proton pump. This produces a proton gradient more directly, which is then converted to chemical energy. The process does not involve carbon dioxide fixation and does not release oxygen, and seems to have evolved separately from the more common types of photosynthesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Photosynthetic_membranes_and_organelles">Photosynthetic membranes and organelles</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chloroplast.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="732" data-file-width="1445" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Chloroplast.svg/275px-Chloroplast.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Chloroplast.svg/413px-Chloroplast.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Chloroplast.svg/550px-Chloroplast.svg.png 2x" width="275"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chloroplast.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><b>Chloroplast ultrastructure</b>:<br/>  1. outer membrane<br/>  2. intermembrane space<br/>  3. inner membrane (1+2+3: envelope)<br/>  4. stroma (aqueous fluid)<br/>  5. thylakoid lumen (inside of thylakoid)<br/>  6. thylakoid membrane<br/>  7. granum (stack of thylakoids)<br/>  8. thylakoid (lamella)<br/>  9. starch<br/>  10. ribosome<br/>  11. plastidial DNA<br/>  12. plastoglobule (drop of lipids)</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">Chloroplast</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thylakoid" title="Thylakoid">Thylakoid</a></div>
<p>In photosynthetic bacteria, the proteins that gather light for photosynthesis are embedded in <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membranes</a>. In its simplest form, this involves the membrane surrounding the cell itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17055774_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17055774-20">[20]</a></sup> However, the membrane may be tightly folded into cylindrical sheets called <a href="/wiki/Thylakoid" title="Thylakoid">thylakoids</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mullineaux1999_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mullineaux1999-21">[21]</a></sup> or bunched up into round <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(biology)" title="Vesicle (biology)">vesicles</a> called <i>intracytoplasmic membranes</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17895378_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17895378-22">[22]</a></sup> These structures can fill most of the interior of a cell, giving the membrane a very large surface area and therefore increasing the amount of light that the bacteria can absorb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mullineaux1999_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mullineaux1999-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>In plants and algae, photosynthesis takes place in <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelles</a> called <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>. A typical <a href="/wiki/Plant_cell" title="Plant cell">plant cell</a> contains about 10 to 100 chloroplasts. The chloroplast is enclosed by a membrane. This membrane is composed of a phospholipid inner membrane, a phospholipid outer membrane, and an intermembrane space. Enclosed by the membrane is an aqueous fluid called the stroma. Embedded within the stroma are stacks of thylakoids (grana), which are the site of photosynthesis. The thylakoids appear as flattened disks. The thylakoid itself is enclosed by the thylakoid membrane, and within the enclosed volume is a lumen or thylakoid space. Embedded in the thylakoid membrane are integral and <a href="/wiki/Peripheral_membrane_protein" title="Peripheral membrane protein">peripheral membrane protein</a> complexes of the photosynthetic system.
</p><p>Plants absorb light primarily using the <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a>. The green part of the light spectrum is not absorbed but is reflected which is the reason that most plants have a green color. Besides chlorophyll, plants also use pigments such as <a href="/wiki/Carotene" title="Carotene">carotenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xanthophyll" title="Xanthophyll">xanthophylls</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-13-250882-6_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-13-250882-6-23">[23]</a></sup> Algae also use chlorophyll, but various other pigments are present, such as <a href="/wiki/Phycocyanin" title="Phycocyanin">phycocyanin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carotene" title="Carotene">carotenes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Xanthophyll" title="Xanthophyll">xanthophylls</a> in <a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">green algae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phycoerythrin" title="Phycoerythrin">phycoerythrin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">red algae</a> (rhodophytes) and <a href="/wiki/Fucoxanthin" title="Fucoxanthin">fucoxanthin</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brown_algae" title="Brown algae">brown algae</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatoms" title="Diatoms">diatoms</a> resulting in a wide variety of colors.
</p><p>These pigments are embedded in plants and algae in complexes called antenna proteins. In such proteins, the pigments are arranged to work together. Such a combination of proteins is also called a <a href="/wiki/Light-harvesting_complex" title="Light-harvesting complex">light-harvesting complex</a>.
</p><p>Although all cells in the green parts of a plant have chloroplasts, the majority of those are found in specially adapted structures called <a href="/wiki/Leaf" title="Leaf">leaves</a>. Certain species adapted to conditions of strong sunlight and <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">aridity</a>, such as many <a href="/wiki/Euphorbia" title="Euphorbia">Euphorbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cactus" title="Cactus">cactus</a> species, have their main photosynthetic organs in their stems. The cells in the interior tissues of a leaf, called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesophyll_tissue" title="Mesophyll tissue">mesophyll</a>, can contain between 450,000 and 800,000 chloroplasts for every square millimeter of leaf. The surface of the leaf is coated with a water-resistant <a href="/wiki/Wax" title="Wax">waxy</a> <a href="/wiki/Plant_cuticle" title="Plant cuticle">cuticle</a> that protects the leaf from excessive <a href="/wiki/Evaporation" title="Evaporation">evaporation</a> of water and decreases the absorption of <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> or <a href="/wiki/Blue" title="Blue">blue</a> <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> to reduce <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heating</a>. The transparent <a href="/wiki/Leaf#Epidermis" title="Leaf">epidermis</a> layer allows light to pass through to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesophyll_tissue" title="Mesophyll tissue">palisade</a> mesophyll cells where most of the photosynthesis takes place.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Light-dependent_reactions">Light-dependent reactions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="906" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg/450px-Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg/675px-Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg/900px-Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg.png 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thylakoid_membrane_3.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Light-dependent reactions of photosynthesis at the thylakoid membrane</div></div></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Light-dependent_reactions" title="Light-dependent reactions">Light-dependent reactions</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Light-dependent_reactions" title="Light-dependent reactions">light-dependent reactions</a>, one molecule of the <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigment</a> <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> absorbs one <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photon</a> and loses one <a href="/wiki/Electron" title="Electron">electron</a>. This electron is passed to a modified form of chlorophyll called <a href="/wiki/Pheophytin" title="Pheophytin">pheophytin</a>, which passes the electron to a <a href="/wiki/Quinone" title="Quinone">quinone</a> molecule, starting the flow of electrons down an <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a> that leads to the ultimate reduction of <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide_phosphate" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate">NADP</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide_phosphate" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate">NADPH</a>. In addition, this creates a <a href="/wiki/Electrochemical_gradient" title="Electrochemical gradient">proton gradient</a> (energy gradient) across the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast_membrane" title="Chloroplast membrane">chloroplast membrane</a>, which is used by <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a> in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a>. The chlorophyll molecule ultimately regains the electron it lost when a water molecule is split in a process called <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">photolysis</a>, which releases a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dioxygen" title="Dioxygen">dioxygen</a> (O<sub>2</sub>) molecule as a waste product.
</p><p>The overall equation for the light-dependent reactions under the conditions of non-cyclic electron flow in green plants is:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raven-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 NADP<sup>+</sup> + 3 ADP + 3 P<sub>i</sub> + light → 2 NADPH + 2 H<sup>+</sup> + 3 ATP + O<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Not all <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of light can support photosynthesis. The photosynthetic action spectrum depends on the type of <a href="/wiki/Accessory_pigment" title="Accessory pigment">accessory pigments</a> present. For example, in green plants, the <a href="/wiki/Action_spectrum" title="Action spectrum">action spectrum</a> resembles the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_spectrum" title="Absorption spectrum">absorption spectrum</a> for <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophylls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> with absorption peaks in violet-blue and red light. In red algae, the action spectrum  is blue-green light, which allows these algae to use the blue end of the spectrum to grow in the deeper waters that filter out the longer wavelengths (red light) used by above ground green plants. The non-absorbed part of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">light spectrum</a> is what gives photosynthetic organisms their color (e.g., green plants, red algae, purple bacteria) and is the least effective for photosynthesis in the respective organisms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Z_scheme">Z scheme</span></h3>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:677px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Z-scheme.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="281" data-file-width="905" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Z-scheme.png/675px-Z-scheme.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Z-scheme.png 1.5x" width="675"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Z-scheme.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "Z scheme"</div></div></div></div>
<p>In plants, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light-dependent_reaction" title="Light-dependent reaction">light-dependent reactions</a> occur in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thylakoid_membrane" title="Thylakoid membrane">thylakoid membranes</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a> where they drive the synthesis of ATP and NADPH. The light-dependent reactions are of two forms: cyclic and non-cyclic.
</p><p>In the non-cyclic reaction, the <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">photons</a> are captured in the light-harvesting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_complex" title="Antenna complex">antenna complexes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Photosystem" title="Photosystem">photosystem II</a> by <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accessory_pigments" title="Accessory pigments">accessory pigments</a> (see diagram at right). The absorption of a photon by the antenna complex frees an electron by a process called <a href="/wiki/Photoinduced_charge_separation" title="Photoinduced charge separation">photoinduced charge separation</a>. The antenna system is at the core of the chlorophyll molecule of the photosystem II reaction center. That freed electron is transferred to the primary electron-acceptor molecule, pheophytin. As the electrons are shuttled through an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_transfer_chain" title="Electron transfer chain">electron transport chain</a> (the so-called <i><b>Z-scheme</b></i> shown in the diagram), it initially functions to generate a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemiosmotic_potential" title="Chemiosmotic potential">chemiosmotic potential</a> by pumping proton cations (H<sup>+</sup>) across the membrane and into the thylakoid space. An <a href="/wiki/ATP_synthase" title="ATP synthase">ATP synthase</a> enzyme uses that chemiosmotic potential to make ATP during <a href="/wiki/Photophosphorylation" title="Photophosphorylation">photophosphorylation</a>, whereas <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADPH" title="NADPH">NADPH</a> is a product of the terminal <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction in the <i>Z-scheme</i>. The electron enters a chlorophyll molecule in <a href="/wiki/Photosystem_I" title="Photosystem I">Photosystem I</a>. There it is further excited by the light absorbed by that <a href="/wiki/Photosystem" title="Photosystem">photosystem</a>. The electron is then passed along a chain of <a href="/wiki/Electron_acceptor" title="Electron acceptor">electron acceptors</a> to which it transfers some of its energy. The energy delivered to the electron acceptors is used to move hydrogen ions across the thylakoid membrane into the lumen. The electron is eventually used to reduce the co-enzyme NADP with a H<sup>+</sup> to NADPH (which has functions in the light-independent reaction); at that point, the path of that electron ends.
</p><p>The cyclic reaction is similar to that of the non-cyclic, but differs in that it generates only ATP, and no reduced NADP (NADPH) is created. The cyclic reaction takes place only at photosystem I. Once the electron is displaced from the photosystem, the electron is passed down the electron acceptor molecules and returns to photosystem I, from where it was emitted, hence the name <i>cyclic reaction</i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_photolysis">Water photolysis</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Photodissociation" title="Photodissociation">Photodissociation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_evolution" title="Oxygen evolution">Oxygen evolution</a></div>
<p>The NADPH is the main <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a> produced by chloroplasts, which then goes on to provide a source of energetic electrons in other cellular reactions. Its production leaves chlorophyll in photosystem I with a deficit of electrons (chlorophyll has been oxidized), which must be balanced by some other reducing agent that will supply the missing electron. The excited electrons lost from chlorophyll from <a href="/wiki/Photosystem_I" title="Photosystem I">photosystem I</a> are supplied from the electron transport chain by <a href="/wiki/Plastocyanin" title="Plastocyanin">plastocyanin</a>. However, since <a href="/wiki/Photosystem_II" title="Photosystem II">photosystem II</a> is the first step of the <i>Z-scheme</i>, an external source of electrons is required to reduce its oxidized <b>chlorophyll <i>a</i></b> molecules. The source of electrons in green-plant and cyanobacterial photosynthesis is water. Two water molecules are oxidized by four successive charge-separation reactions by photosystem II to yield a molecule of diatomic <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and four <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> ions; the electrons yielded are transferred to a redox-active <a href="/wiki/Tyrosine" title="Tyrosine">tyrosine</a> residue that then reduces the oxidized chlorophyll <i>a</i> (called P680) that serves as the primary light-driven electron donor in the photosystem II reaction center. That photo receptor is in effect reset and is then able to repeat the absorption of another photon and the release of another photo-dissociated electron. The oxidation of water is <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyzed</a> in photosystem II by a redox-active structure that contains four <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a> ions and a calcium ion; this <a href="/wiki/Oxygen-evolving_complex" title="Oxygen-evolving complex">oxygen-evolving complex</a> binds two water molecules and contains the four oxidizing equivalents that are used to drive the water-oxidizing reaction (Dolai's S-state diagrams).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Photosystem II is the only known biological <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that carries out this oxidation of water. The hydrogen ions released contribute to the transmembrane chemiosmotic potential that leads to ATP synthesis. Oxygen is a waste product of light-dependent reactions, but the majority of organisms on Earth use oxygen for <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>, including photosynthetic organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VK_Yachandra_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-VK_Yachandra-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18250316_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18250316-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Light-independent_reactions">Light-independent reactions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calvin_cycle">Calvin cycle</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Light-independent_reactions" title="Light-independent reactions">Light-independent reactions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">Carbon fixation</a></div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Light-independent_reactions" title="Light-independent reactions">light-independent</a> (or "dark") reactions, the <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> <a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">RuBisCO</a> captures <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a> and, in a process called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvin-Benson_cycle" title="Calvin-Benson cycle">Calvin-Benson cycle</a>, it uses the newly formed NADPH and releases three-carbon sugars, which are later combined to form sucrose and starch. The overall equation for the light-independent reactions in green plants is<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raven_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raven-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>128</span></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>3 CO<sub>2</sub> + 9 ATP + 6 NADPH + 6 H<sup>+</sup> → C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>3</sub>-phosphate + 9 ADP + 8 P<sub>i</sub> + 6 NADP<sup>+</sup> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calvin-cycle4.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="766" data-file-width="836" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Calvin-cycle4.svg/400px-Calvin-cycle4.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Calvin-cycle4.svg/600px-Calvin-cycle4.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Calvin-cycle4.svg/800px-Calvin-cycle4.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calvin-cycle4.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of the Calvin cycle and carbon fixation</div></div></div>
<p>Carbon fixation produces the intermediate three-carbon sugar product, which is then converted to the final carbohydrate products. The simple carbon sugars produced by photosynthesis are then used in the forming of other organic compounds, such as the building material <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, the precursors for <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> biosynthesis, or as a fuel in <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>. The latter occurs not only in plants but also in <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a> when the energy from plants is passed through a <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>.
</p><p>The fixation or reduction of carbon dioxide is a process in which <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> combines with a five-carbon sugar, <a href="/wiki/Ribulose_1,5-bisphosphate" title="Ribulose 1,5-bisphosphate">ribulose 1,5-bisphosphate</a>, to yield two molecules of a three-carbon compound, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glycerate_3-phosphate" title="Glycerate 3-phosphate">glycerate 3-phosphate</a>, also known as 3-phosphoglycerate. Glycerate 3-phosphate, in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADPH" title="NADPH">NADPH</a> produced during the light-dependent stages, is reduced to <a href="/wiki/Glyceraldehyde_3-phosphate" title="Glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate">glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate</a>. This product is also referred to as 3-phosphoglyceraldehyde (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PGAL" title="PGAL">PGAL</a>) or, more generically, as <a href="/wiki/Triose" title="Triose">triose</a> phosphate. Most (5 out of 6 molecules) of the glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate produced is used to regenerate ribulose 1,5-bisphosphate so the process can continue. The triose phosphates not thus "recycled" often condense to form <a href="/wiki/Hexose" title="Hexose">hexose</a> phosphates, which ultimately yield <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>. The sugars produced during carbon <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a> yield carbon skeletons that can be used for other metabolic reactions like the production of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipids" title="Lipids">lipids</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_concentrating_mechanisms">Carbon concentrating mechanisms</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="On_land">On land</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HatchSlackpathway2.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="705" height="550" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/HatchSlackpathway2.svg/400px-HatchSlackpathway2.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/HatchSlackpathway2.svg/600px-HatchSlackpathway2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/HatchSlackpathway2.svg/800px-HatchSlackpathway2.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HatchSlackpathway2.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Overview of <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 carbon fixation</a></div></div></div>
<p>In hot and dry conditions, plants close their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomata" title="Stomata">stomata</a> to prevent water loss. Under these conditions, CO<sub>2</sub> will decrease and oxygen gas, produced by the light reactions of photosynthesis, will increase, causing an increase of <a href="/wiki/Photorespiration" title="Photorespiration">photorespiration</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Oxygenase" title="Oxygenase">oxygenase</a> activity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubisco" title="Rubisco">ribulose-1,5-bisphosphate carboxylase/oxygenase</a> and decrease in carbon fixation. Some plants have <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> mechanisms to increase the CO<sub>2</sub> concentration in the leaves under these conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamsjohnston_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-williamsjohnston-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 carbon fixation</a></div>
<p>Plants that use the <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C<sub>4</sub></a> carbon fixation process chemically fix carbon dioxide in the cells of the mesophyll by adding it to the three-carbon molecule <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphoenolpyruvate" title="Phosphoenolpyruvate">phosphoenolpyruvate (PEP)</a>, a reaction catalyzed by an enzyme called <a href="/wiki/Phosphoenolpyruvate_carboxylase" title="Phosphoenolpyruvate carboxylase">PEP carboxylase</a>, creating the four-carbon organic acid <a href="/wiki/Oxaloacetic_acid" title="Oxaloacetic acid">oxaloacetic acid</a>. Oxaloacetic acid or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malate" title="Malate">malate</a> synthesized by this process is then translocated to specialized <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bundle_sheath" title="Bundle sheath">bundle sheath</a> cells where the enzyme <a href="/wiki/RuBisCO" title="RuBisCO">RuBisCO</a> and other Calvin cycle enzymes are located, and where CO<sub>2</sub> released by <a href="/wiki/Decarboxylation" title="Decarboxylation">decarboxylation</a> of the four-carbon acids is then fixed by RuBisCO activity to the three-carbon <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-phosphoglyceric_acid" title="3-phosphoglyceric acid">3-phosphoglyceric acids</a>. The physical separation of RuBisCO from the oxygen-generating light reactions reduces photorespiration and increases CO<sub>2</sub> fixation and, thus, the <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_capacity" title="Photosynthetic capacity">photosynthetic capacity</a> of the leaf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taiz_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taiz-29">[29]</a></sup> C<sub>4</sub> plants can produce more sugar than C<sub>3</sub> plants in conditions of high light and temperature. Many important crop plants are C<sub>4</sub> plants, including maize, sorghum, sugarcane, and millet. Plants that do not use PEP-carboxylase in carbon fixation are called <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C<sub>3</sub> plants</a> because the primary carboxylation reaction, catalyzed by RuBisCO, produces the three-carbon 3-phosphoglyceric acids directly in the Calvin-Benson cycle. Over 90% of plants use C<sub>3</sub> carbon fixation, compared to 3% that use C<sub>4</sub> carbon fixation;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sage_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-sage-30">[30]</a></sup> however, the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> in over 60 plant lineages makes it a striking example of <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williamsjohnston_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-williamsjohnston-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAM_photosynthesis" title="CAM photosynthesis">CAM photosynthesis</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xerophytes" title="Xerophytes">Xerophytes</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cacti" title="Cacti">cacti</a> and most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Succulents" title="Succulents">succulents</a>, also use PEP carboxylase to capture carbon dioxide in a process called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CAM_photosynthesis" title="CAM photosynthesis">Crassulacean acid metabolism</a> (CAM). In contrast to C<sub>4</sub> metabolism, which <i>spatially</i> separates the CO<sub>2</sub> fixation to PEP from the Calvin cycle, CAM <i>temporally</i> separates these two processes. CAM plants have a different leaf anatomy from C<sub>3</sub> plants, and fix the CO<sub>2</sub> at night, when their stomata are open. CAM plants store the CO<sub>2</sub> mostly in the form of <a href="/wiki/Malic_acid" title="Malic acid">malic acid</a> via carboxylation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphoenolpyruvate" title="Phosphoenolpyruvate">phosphoenolpyruvate</a> to oxaloacetate, which is then reduced to malate. Decarboxylation of malate during the day releases CO<sub>2</sub> inside the leaves, thus allowing carbon fixation to 3-phosphoglycerate by RuBisCO. Sixteen thousand species of plants use CAM.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11886877_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11886877-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_water">In water</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">Cyanobacteria</a> possess <a href="/wiki/Carboxysome" title="Carboxysome">carboxysomes</a>, which increase the concentration of CO<sub>2</sub> around RuBisCO to increase the rate of photosynthesis. An enzyme, <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_anhydrase" title="Carbonic anhydrase">carbonic anhydrase</a>, located within the carboxysome releases CO<sub>2</sub> from the dissolved hydrocarbonate ions (HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>). Before the CO<sub>2</sub> diffuses out it is quickly sponged up by RuBisCO, which is concentrated within the carboxysomes. HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span> ions are made from CO<sub>2</sub> outside the cell by another carbonic anhydrase and are actively pumped into the cell by a membrane protein. They cannot cross the membrane as they are charged, and within the cytosol they turn back into CO<sub>2</sub> very slowly without the help of carbonic anhydrase. This causes the HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">−</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span> ions to accumulate within the cell from where they diffuse into the carboxysomes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pyrenoid" title="Pyrenoid">Pyrenoids</a> in <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hornwort" title="Hornwort">hornworts</a> also act to concentrate CO<sub>2</sub> around rubisco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Order_and_kinetics">Order and kinetics</span></h2>
<p>The overall process of photosynthesis takes place in four stages:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ps_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ps-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Stage</th>
<th>Description</th>
<th>Time scale
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>1</td>
<td>Energy transfer in antenna chlorophyll (thylakoid membranes)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Femtosecond" title="Femtosecond">femtosecond</a> to <a href="/wiki/Picosecond" title="Picosecond">picosecond</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2</td>
<td>Transfer of electrons in photochemical reactions (thylakoid membranes)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Picosecond" title="Picosecond">picosecond</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nanosecond" title="Nanosecond">nanosecond</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3</td>
<td>Electron transport chain and ATP synthesis (thylakoid membranes)</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">microsecond</a> to <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">millisecond</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>4</td>
<td>Carbon fixation and export of stable products</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">millisecond</a> to <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">second</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Efficiency">Efficiency</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_efficiency" title="Photosynthetic efficiency">Photosynthetic efficiency</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plants</a> usually convert light into <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">chemical energy</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Photosynthetic_efficiency" title="Photosynthetic efficiency">photosynthetic efficiency</a> of 3–6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-url_Chapter_1_–_Biological_energy_production_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-url_Chapter_1_–_Biological_energy_production-34">[34]</a></sup>
Absorbed light that is unconverted is dissipated primarily as heat, with a small fraction (1–2%)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chlorophyll_fluorescence_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chlorophyll_fluorescence-35">[35]</a></sup> re-emitted as <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll_fluorescence" title="Chlorophyll fluorescence">chlorophyll fluorescence</a> at longer (redder) wavelengths. This fact allows measurement of the light reaction of photosynthesis by using chlorophyll fluorometers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Actual plants' photosynthetic efficiency varies with the frequency of the light being converted, light intensity, temperature and proportion of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere, and can vary from 0.1% to 8%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlWhat_is_Photosynthesis_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlWhat_is_Photosynthesis-37">[37]</a></sup> By comparison, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photovoltaic_module" title="Photovoltaic module">solar panels</a> convert light into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_energy" title="Electric energy">electric energy</a> at an efficiency of approximately 6–20% for mass-produced panels, and above 40% in laboratory devices.
</p><p>The efficiency of both light and dark reactions can be measured but the relationship between the two can be complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenqvist_E._2006_pages_39-78_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenqvist_E._2006_pages_39-78-38">[38]</a></sup> For example, the ATP and NADPH energy molecules, created by the light reaction, can be used for carbon fixation or for photorespiration in C<sub>3</sub> plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenqvist_E._2006_pages_39-78_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenqvist_E._2006_pages_39-78-38">[38]</a></sup> Electrons may also flow to other electron sinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9490760_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9490760-41">[41]</a></sup> For this reason, it is not uncommon for authors to differentiate between work done under non-photorespiratory conditions and under photorespiratory conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_N._R._2008_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_N._R._2008-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chlorophyll fluorescence of photosystem II can measure the light reaction, and Infrared gas analyzers can  measure the dark reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernacchi_2002_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernacchi_2002-45">[45]</a></sup> It is also possible to investigate both at the same time using an integrated chlorophyll fluorometer and gas exchange system, or by using two separate systems together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribas-Carbo_2010_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ribas-Carbo_2010-46">[46]</a></sup> Infrared gas analyzers and some  moisture sensors are sensitive enough to measure the photosynthetic assimilation of CO<sub>2</sub>, and of ΔH<sub>2</sub>O using reliable methods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long2003_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-long2003-47">[47]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> is commonly measured in μmols/m<sup>2</sup>/s<sup>−1</sup>, parts per million or volume per million and H<sub>2</sub>0 is commonly measured in mmol/m<sup>2</sup>/s<sup>−1</sup> or in mbars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long2003_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-long2003-47">[47]</a></sup> By measuring CO<sub>2</sub> assimilation, ΔH<sub>2</sub>O, leaf temperature, barometric pressure, leaf area, and photosynthetically active radiation or PAR, it becomes possible to estimate, “A” or carbon assimilation, “E” or transpiration, “gs” or stomatal conductance, and Ci or intracellular CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long2003_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-long2003-47">[47]</a></sup> However, it is more common to used chlorophyll fluorescence for plant stress measurement, where appropriate, because the most commonly used measuring parameters FV/FM and Y(II) or F/FM’ can be made in a few seconds, allowing the measurement of larger plant populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baker_N._R._2008_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baker_N._R._2008-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Gas exchange systems that offer control of CO<sub>2</sub> levels, above and below ambient, allow the common practice of measurement of A/Ci curves, at different CO<sub>2</sub> levels, to characterize a plant’s photosynthetic response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-long2003_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-long2003-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Integrated chlorophyll fluorometer – gas exchange systems allow a more precise measure of photosynthetic response and mechanisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernacchi_2002_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernacchi_2002-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribas-Carbo_2010_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ribas-Carbo_2010-46">[46]</a></sup> While standard gas exchange photosynthesis systems can measure Ci, or substomatal CO<sub>2</sub> levels, the addition of integrated chlorophyll fluorescence measurements allows a more precise measurement of C<sub>C</sub> to replace Ci.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribas-Carbo_2010_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ribas-Carbo_2010-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The estimation of CO<sub>2</sub> at the site of carboxylation in the chloroplast, or C<sub>C</sub>, becomes possible with the measurement of mesophyll conductance or g<sub>m</sub> using an integrated system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernacchi_2002_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bernacchi_2002-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ribas-Carbo_2010_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ribas-Carbo_2010-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Photosynthesis measurement systems are not designed to directly measure the amount of light absorbed by the leaf. But analysis of chlorophyll-fluorescence, P700- and P515-absorbance and gas exchange measurements reveal detailed information about e.g. the photosystems, quantum efficiency and the CO<sub>2</sub> assimilation rates. With some instruments even wavelength-dependency of the photosynthetic efficiency can be analyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>A phenomenon known as <a href="/wiki/Quantum_walk" title="Quantum walk">quantum walk</a> increases the efficiency of the energy transport of light significantly. In the photosynthetic cell of an algae, bacterium, or plant, there are light-sensitive molecules called <a href="/wiki/Chromophore" title="Chromophore">chromophores</a> arranged in an antenna-shaped structure named a photocomplex. When a photon is absorbed by a chromophore, it is converted into a <a href="/wiki/Quasiparticle" title="Quasiparticle">quasiparticle</a> referred to as an <a href="/wiki/Exciton" title="Exciton">exciton</a>, which jumps from chromophore to chromophore towards the reaction center of the photocomplex, a collection of molecules that traps its energy in a chemical form that makes it accessible for the cell's metabolism. The exciton's wave properties enable it to cover a wider area and try out several possible paths simultaneously, allowing it to instantaneously "choose" the most efficient route, where it will have the highest probability of arriving at its destination in the minimum possible time. Because that quantum walking takes place at temperatures far higher than quantum phenomena usually occur, it is only possible over very short distances, due to obstacles in the form of destructive interference that come into play. These obstacles cause the particle to lose its wave properties for an instant before it regains them once again after it is freed from its locked position through a classic "hop". The movement of the electron towards the photo center is therefore covered in a series of conventional hops and quantum walks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlBBC_News_-_Plants_seen_doing_quantum_physics_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlBBC_News_-_Plants_seen_doing_quantum_physics-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-urlQuantum_Biology:_Better_Living_Through_Quantum_Mechanics_-_The_Nature_of_Reality_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-urlQuantum_Biology:_Better_Living_Through_Quantum_Mechanics_-_The_Nature_of_Reality-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23788794_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23788794-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
